  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4933524c-82ae-41c7-8430-40570d2522d1'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

  <span id="freeText9038453070729634718" style="display:none"><i>An alternate cover edition of ISBN 978-0385543781 can be found <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/54245898-the-testaments" rel="nofollow noopener">here.</a></i><br /><br />When the van door slammed on Offred's future at the end of <i>The Handmaid's Tale</i>, readers had no way of telling what lay ahead for her--freedom, prison or death.<br /><br />With <i>The Testaments</i>, the wait is over.<br /><br />Margaret Atwood's sequel picks up the story more than fifteen years after Offred stepped into the unknown, with the explosive testaments of three female narrators from Gilead.<br /><br />In this brilliant sequel to <i>The Handmaid's Tale, </i>acclaimed author Margaret Atwood answers the questions that have tantalized readers for decades.<br /><br />"Dear Readers: Everything you've ever asked me about Gilead and its inner workings is the inspiration for this book. <span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    well wouldn't you choose a relatively-sheltered teenager to whom you've never even really spoken, whose presumptive parents had just been murdered, to<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    well wouldn't you choose a relatively-sheltered teenager to whom you've never even really spoken, whose presumptive parents had just been murdered, to take on an extremely dangerous and very important mission for which she's entirely unprepared? I mean, obviously the right choice. Good thing it only takes a couple days of training to learn to punch people exactly right.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>

<div id="review_2608733195" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2608733195" />
    <a title="Tatiana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana"><img alt="Tatiana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1249783659p2/2250020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2608733195?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tatiana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tatiana" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana">Tatiana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">big fans of the TV show version</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=dystopias-post-apocalyptic">dystopias-post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=i-am-so-over-you">i-am-so-over-you</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=why-the-hype">why-the-hype</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=booker">booker</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2608733195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5973844526416164548">I guess I'll have to be the one who says what nobody else is willing to say. <b>This novel is terrible, and Booker judges are starstruck, hype-driven sellouts. And that no professional literary critic has the guts to tell the truth about how poorly conceived and written <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments' title='The Testaments by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Testaments</a> is, is a true shame. </b> <br/><br/>I can't decide which work Atwood should be embarrassed for more - <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28146168.Angel_Catbird__Vol__1__Angel_Catbird___1_' title='Angel Catbird, Vol. 1 (Angel Catbird, #1) by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>Angel Catbird, Vol. 1</a> or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments' title='The Testaments by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Testaments</a>. The book doesn't read like a novel written by one of the most lauded authors of the 20st</span>
  <span id="freeText5973844526416164548" style="display:none">I guess I'll have to be the one who says what nobody else is willing to say. <b>This novel is terrible, and Booker judges are starstruck, hype-driven sellouts. And that no professional literary critic has the guts to tell the truth about how poorly conceived and written <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments" title="The Testaments by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> is, is a true shame. </b> <br /><br />I can't decide which work Atwood should be embarrassed for more - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28146168.Angel_Catbird__Vol__1__Angel_Catbird___1_" title="Angel Catbird, Vol. 1 (Angel Catbird, #1) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">Angel Catbird, Vol. 1</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments" title="The Testaments by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a>. The book doesn't read like a novel written by one of the most lauded authors of the 20st century. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments" title="The Testaments by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> reads like a standard-issue feminist YA dystopia, filled with every overused dystopian trope and every stereotype, penned by an author who writes for teen audience, and is published by Harper Teen. As such, it undoubtedly has some appeal to a part of Atwood's readership, but literary merit <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments" title="The Testaments by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> has none. If this book had a different name on it, I would have DNFed it after 50 pages for its lack of originality, predictability and mediocre writing style. I’ve read a fair number of similar novels, I am not opposed to them, I enjoyed some of them, and some of them (for example the upcoming <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43263520.The_Grace_Year" title="The Grace Year by Kim Liggett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Grace Year</a>) held my attention much better. As an Atwood novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments" title="The Testaments by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> gets one disappointed, angry, heart-broken star from me. <br /><br />Why did the publishers embargo Atwood's new creation, I wonder? Surely there is nothing to spoil. Maybe to conceal its poor execution, or its transparent, shallow, simplistic, and ridiculous plot? <br /><br />The story is told from 3 POVs - Aunt Lydia's and two teen girls' - one growing up in Gilead and another - in Canada. Oh my, who those girls might be? Twist! <br /><br />You can only glimpse Atwood's former brilliance in Aunt Lydia's POV, but just for a few moments here and there. If the whole novel was written about Aunt Lydia, maybe Atwood would have made her journey more convincing, but alas. The other two girls are quintessential YA dystopian heroines - one abused by an evil oppressive regime, and the other - a bratty teen on the run from bad people, but who nevertheless has time for some romance. Yum! Like I said, these POVs are so similar to what's been regurgitated over and over in teen publishing, it's uncanny. Has <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments" title="The Testaments by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> been partially ghost-written by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2936493.Lauren_Oliver" title="Lauren Oliver" rel="nofollow noopener">Lauren Oliver</a>? I am not trying to dump on Oliver, she has her fans and her place in the industry, but I expected something infinitely more sophisticated from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood" title="Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">Margaret Atwood</a>.<br /><br />The new information about Gilead Atwood promised? Well, new details of Gilead made the regime more nonsensical and less plausible than ever before. Who benefits from living in Gilead becomes unfathomable in this book, thus making the entire concept pointless (kind of like in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8525590.Wither__The_Chemical_Garden___1_" title="Wither (The Chemical Garden, #1) by Lauren DeStefano" rel="nofollow noopener">Wither</a>). Totalitarian regimes work, at least temporarily, because they are supported by a mass of true believers. Where are they in this book? But if you yearn for some more torture porn in addition to that supplied by the 3 seasons of the TV show, then sure. Rapists, molesters, killers, suicide victims galore, plus solitary confinement - there is an overabundance of that. All of it written bluntly, rashly, exploitatively and without any kind of nuance. There is no overarching theme in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments" title="The Testaments by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> except GILEAD IS BAD. The plot to overthrow it is a joke. <br /><br />If you are looking for a clever, thoughtful, well-written companion to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a>, you are out of luck. If you want a bland, basic TV show fanfic stuffed with action adventure and genre tropes, enjoy! <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments" title="The Testaments by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> was written just for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5973844526416164548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2609029824" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2609029824" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2609029824?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781784742324" href="/book/show/42973319-the-testaments">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2609029824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14437910857673722635">I can sum it up simply: <b>this book is not needed.</b> <br/><br/>I hoped that wouldn't be the case. I really <i>really</i> hoped Atwood had something important to add to the world of Gilead with this book, but she honestly doesn't. If anything, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments__The_Handmaid_s_Tale___2_' title='The Testaments (The Handmaid's Tale, #2) by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Testaments</a> serves only to weaken the power of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale' title='The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Handmaid's Tale</a>.<br/><br/>In the past, I have spoken highly of authors who are not afraid to "be evil" with their books. This may give the impression that they are doing something particularly nefarious, but, in fact, it’s not so much</span>
  <span id="freeText14437910857673722635" style="display:none">I can sum it up simply: <b>this book is not needed.</b> <br /><br />I hoped that wouldn't be the case. I really <i>really</i> hoped Atwood had something important to add to the world of Gilead with this book, but she honestly doesn't. If anything, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments__The_Handmaid_s_Tale___2_" title="The Testaments (The Handmaid's Tale, #2) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> serves only to weaken the power of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a>.<br /><br />In the past, I have spoken highly of authors who are not afraid to "be evil" with their books. This may give the impression that they are doing something particularly nefarious, but, in fact, it’s not so much something they do, but everything they don’t. It’s an act of self-restraint to not say everything, to leave some things unanswered, some happy endings unexplored. That, I feel, is one of the greatest strengths of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a>.<br /><br />Because there is so much we don't know; can't know. Everything we experience comes from Offred's narrow world view. Everything Offred doesn't know-- we don't know. The ending, too, is famously ambiguous. And these are extremely powerful tools. What we don’t know is powerful. Ambiguity is powerful. Knowing when to finish is powerful. As Aunt Lydia notes herself in this very book:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Where there is emptiness, the mind will obligingly fill it up. Fear is always at hand to supply any vacancies, as is curiosity.</i>
</blockquote><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a> forces us to wonder, to imagine, to fear the worst and hope for the best. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments__The_Handmaid_s_Tale___2_" title="The Testaments (The Handmaid's Tale, #2) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> not so much. <br /><br />What this book does is remove the ambiguity. It provides answers to thirty-five year old mysteries that were best left unanswered. I am reminded somewhat comically of Jojo Moyes' inability to let go of her <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17347634.Me_Before_You__Me_Before_You___1_" title="Me Before You (Me Before You, #1) by Jojo Moyes" rel="nofollow noopener">Me Before You</a> characters, repeatedly opening up the story after leaving it on an emotional high. Not every "ooh, I wonder what the characters did next?" should be answered. Sometimes not knowing is so much more effective. And that's Moyes. I didn't expect Atwood to indulge in this sentimentality. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a> uses one limited perspective to make us think; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975172.The_Testaments__The_Handmaid_s_Tale___2_" title="The Testaments (The Handmaid's Tale, #2) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a> uses three perspectives and an epilogue in the future to colour in all the corners, leaving nothing to the imagination. <br /><br />I gave this book two stars for Aunt Lydia's perspective. Without her contribution I am honestly not sure I would have pushed through the second half of the book. The rest of the book is told from the perspective of two teenage girls, one living in Canada and the other in Gilead, and the "twists" regarding them are so glaringly obvious that it is actually a bit embarrassing to read the scenes with the dramatic reveals (chapter cliffhanger obviously). The whole infiltration by the resistance thing was straight out of every other dime a dozen dystopia.<br /><br />I had so hoped this was going to do something new and important. I hoped it was going to impart a new message, perhaps relevant to modern times. I hoped it was going to be smart and thought-provoking. I am disappointed. <br /><br /><a href="http://emilymay.me" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.facebook.com/thebookgeekreviews" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/thebookgeek01" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://instagram.com/emleemay" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/c/EmilyMayTheBookGeek" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14437910857673722635" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2609090306" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2609090306" />
    <a title="Marchpane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6677659-marchpane"><img alt="Marchpane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483537315p2/6677659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2609090306?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marchpane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marchpane" href="/user/show/6677659-marchpane">Marchpane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6677659-marchpane?shelf=2019-releases">2019-releases</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2609090306" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5131781383774899473"><b>Return to Gilead</b><br/><br/>Check your expectations at the door: <i>
  <b>The Testaments</b>
</i> is a highly entertaining page turner, but it is also probably quite different from whatever you were anticipating.<br/><br/>It differs from its 1985 antecedent, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale__The_Handmaid_s_Tale___1_' title='The Handmaid's Tale (The Handmaid's Tale, #1) by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Handmaid's Tale</a>, in tone, voice and literary heft. That earlier book had a power and a gravitas that is not recaptured here. For me the most striking thing about <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i> has always been Atwood’s choice of narrator. Offred (in the book she has no other name) is </span>
  <span id="freeText5131781383774899473" style="display:none"><b>Return to Gilead</b><br /><br />Check your expectations at the door: <i>
  <b>The Testaments</b>
</i> is a highly entertaining page turner, but it is also probably quite different from whatever you were anticipating.<br /><br />It differs from its 1985 antecedent, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale__The_Handmaid_s_Tale___1_" title="The Handmaid's Tale (The Handmaid's Tale, #1) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a>, in tone, voice and literary heft. That earlier book had a power and a gravitas that is not recaptured here. For me the most striking thing about <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i> has always been Atwood’s choice of narrator. Offred (in the book she has no other name) is so confined by her circumstances; her isolation is claustrophobic. She is essentially passive, keeping her head down and daring to aim only for survival, while other characters have more agency (Moira and Ofglen both find proactive ways to thwart the Gilead regime, either would have been a more natural choice for a protagonist). Offred is an Everywoman – with her passivity she confronts the reader:<i> Well, what would you do in my place? And don't kid yourself. </i>It's bleak, but the novel's power is in its intimate portrait of powerlessness. <br /><br /><i>The Testaments</i> is more action-driven, more hopeful, and by extension, less realistic. We follow three characters who <i>are</i> prepared to buck the system, to risk everything to crush the patriarchy. That two of them are teenagers feeds the sense of buoyancy, you get the feeling that Atwood too thinks the kids are gonna save us.<br /><br />The shift in tone will be familiar to viewers of Hulu's TV series — perhaps both Atwood and the showrunners 'read the room' and recognised that the catharsis of a feelgood fightback is what we crave and need most right now. If the idea of a book set in Gilead being entertaining — even fun — dismays you, best skip this one. <br /><br />Indeed <i>The Testaments</i>, rather shrewdly on Atwood's part, functions as a sequel to both the first book AND the TV adaptation — deftly combining elements from each, while avoiding the show's most glaring faults (eg its over-reliance on a single character, and tendency to get bogged down plot-wise).<br /><br />This novel isn't flawless either. One of the narrative voices is by far more compelling than the others (no prizes for guessing that it's the mature, morally compromised Aunt Lydia, not one of the idealistic teenagers). It's a little too TV-ready in the way the characters intersect. Certain plot twists are loudly telegraphed and the narratives don't always jive with the historical documents they purport to be. And my eyes rolled more than once (Underground Femaleroad, really?).<br /><br />Still there's much to enjoy. The conniving duplicity and monstrous ambivalence of Aunt Lydia makes for thrilling reading. Atwood's prose and story-spinning have lost none of their magic, and for an 80 year old she writes teenage voices surprisingly well! Most importantly, it's compulsively readable. <br /><br /><i>The Testaments</i> is unlikely to become a perennially relevant classic like its predecessor, and it's unreasonable to expect that kind of greatness from it. As an expansion of the Gilead mythos though, it more than satisfies.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5131781383774899473" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5719954986834816367">This was my most anticipated book for 2019.<br/><br/>Wait... I should amend that statement...<br/><br/>THIS WAS THE MOST ANTICIPATED BOOK OF MY MISERABLE FRIGGIN LIFE!<br/><br/>And much like my life, it was an epic disappointment.<br/><br/>The Handmaid's Tale is on my Top 10 shelf. It is, in my opinion, the greatest dystopian novel of all time. It is everything you expect from the genre and more. Shocking, terrifying, an unflinching account of a fucking nightmarish scenario that could actually happen. <br/><br/>At the end of The Handmaid's Tale</span>
  <span id="freeText5719954986834816367" style="display:none">This was my most anticipated book for 2019.<br /><br />Wait... I should amend that statement...<br /><br />THIS WAS THE MOST ANTICIPATED BOOK OF MY MISERABLE FRIGGIN LIFE!<br /><br />And much like my life, it was an epic disappointment.<br /><br />The Handmaid's Tale is on my Top 10 shelf. It is, in my opinion, the greatest dystopian novel of all time. It is everything you expect from the genre and more. Shocking, terrifying, an unflinching account of a fucking nightmarish scenario that could actually happen. <br /><br />At the end of The Handmaid's Tale I was left devastated and bereft of words. I loved the ambiguity and found myself never wanting to know what happened to June. I consider this decision by Atwood to be the crowning achievement of the novel.<br /><br />With The Testaments Atwood took that crown and crushed it beneath her pen.<br /><br />Obliterated it.<br /><br />And nearly took The Handmaid's Tale with it.</span>

<div id="review_2972275175" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2972275175" />
    <a title="Justin Tate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23082940-justin-tate"><img alt="Justin Tate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593157920p2/23082940.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2972275175?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin Tate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin Tate" href="/user/show/23082940-justin-tate">Justin Tate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2972275175" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8471690453983953939">It's not easy being the most anticipated book of the year. I would argue that most of the negative reactions - including my own - are based largely on expectations. Since published in 1985, The Handmaid's Tale has become sacred ground in the literary world; a true modern classic further amplified by the successful show and current political tensions. Stakes for a sequel couldn't be higher and, even for the ever-talented Margaret Atwood, that's a tough performance to deliver. All in all, this is </span>
  <span id="freeText8471690453983953939" style="display:none">It's not easy being the most anticipated book of the year. I would argue that most of the negative reactions - including my own - are based largely on expectations. Since published in 1985, The Handmaid's Tale has become sacred ground in the literary world; a true modern classic further amplified by the successful show and current political tensions. Stakes for a sequel couldn't be higher and, even for the ever-talented Margaret Atwood, that's a tough performance to deliver. All in all, this is a well-written adventure story that expands the world building hinted at and alluded to by the original. But it's also boring, mostly unsurprising, and generally feels like a cash-in opportunity.<br /><br />More specifically, I was turned off by all the young characters. About 67% of the book is narrated by youth. Their lack of maturity creates a Middle Grade narrative voice that is jarring and undesired. Not necessarily unrealistic, just annoying. Their kiddish thoughts go on for pages and pages when a few brief lines would have sufficed to assure us these characters are indeed children. <br /><br />Another downer is how many questions this book doesn't answer. The original ended with such a dramatic cliffhanger, but the unresolved threads there remain largely unresolved here. I won't say which ones to keep this spoiler-free, but it's important to read this book with more appropriate expectations than what was set up by the publisher. You won't get all of your burning questions answered. Probably not any of them.<br /><br />As for its positive attributes, the few sections narrated by Aunt Lydia are truly spectacular. Aunt Lydia has always been a captivating villain and pulling back the curtain on her thoughts is endlessly intriguing. Listeners of the audiobook are in for an additional treat, since they brought in Ann Dowd from the show to reprise her role for the reading.<br /><br />Again, all in all, this is a decent yarn. It's not going to be a classic, but it's an okay pop novel. I knew pretty much from the first chapter that it wasn't going to deliver everything I desired for a sequel and by a quarter of the way in it was pretty clear what the ending surprise would be, but it still moved at a good pace and kept me moderately in suspense. For Handmaid’s Tale fans, as long as you lower your expectations there's no reason why you can't find enjoyment in these further adventures of Gilead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8471690453983953939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2608823081" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2608823081" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2608823081?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781784742324" href="/book/show/42973319-the-testaments">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=margaret-atwood">margaret-atwood</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2608823081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10664695810684827526">"How tedious is a tyranny in the throes of enactment. It's always the same plot."<br/> <br/>So why does Margaret Atwood choose to tell the story again? That question has haunted me since I heard the announcement of the project almost a year ago. WHY? I was convinced I would hate the hype and the gushing reviews and the book itself, and started reading with the attitude of someone who knew the story didn't need telling again.<br/><br/>To my surprise, I liked it from the start, and soon engaged in the thriller unfold</span>
  <span id="freeText10664695810684827526" style="display:none">"How tedious is a tyranny in the throes of enactment. It's always the same plot."<br /> <br />So why does Margaret Atwood choose to tell the story again? That question has haunted me since I heard the announcement of the project almost a year ago. WHY? I was convinced I would hate the hype and the gushing reviews and the book itself, and started reading with the attitude of someone who knew the story didn't need telling again.<br /><br />To my surprise, I liked it from the start, and soon engaged in the thriller unfolding with a rare reading joy, still wondering why Atwood wasted her precious time on a sequel like this. Not much new was added to the dystopian plot of Gilead, I thought. <br /><br />But then a shift occurred in my mind.<br /><br />I always saw <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a> as a warning of the slow normalisation of religious fanaticism and of the strengthening of patriarchal structures in our modern era, and the story itself as a mirror of our all too human tendencies to adjust to the most absurd situations if we are caught off-guard and left confused.<br /><br />The Testaments has a different purpose, and it comes as a challenge in the era of #MeToo. Don't accept the unacceptable. Act on injustice. Speak up. Do what has to be done to make the world safer for women and children. Say no to the objectification of your body. No tyranny will last forever if you are brave enough to do your individual bit.<br /><br />Whenever Aunt Lydia's badly executed statue was mentioned, I had this strange feeling that it was some kind of Atwoodian insider joke, but I could not put my finger on the reference. In the end I gave up trying to figure out what she meant, as I found my own truth. And I had to wait until the very end: the last page made me laugh out loud. <br /><br />Another scientific conference on Gileadean Studies, another reflection on the difficulty to find truth in details. Truth is in the symbol though, and that noseless, broken statue of Aunt Lydia that was found after the breakdown of Gilead spoke of the ephemeral immortality (deliberately oxymoronic from the start) that Ozimandias fell victim to in Shelley's famous sonnet. I will close with him, as he speaks of the timelessness of power(lessness) and (im)mutability:<br /><br />"I met a traveller from an antique land,<br />Who said—“Two vast and trunkless legs of stone<br />Stand in the desert. . . . Near them, on the sand,<br />Half sunk a shattered visage lies, whose frown,<br />And wrinkled lip, and sneer of cold command,<br />Tell that its sculptor well those passions read<br />Which yet survive, stamped on these lifeless things,<br />The hand that mocked them, and the heart that fed;<br />And on the pedestal, these words appear:<br />My name is Ozymandias, King of Kings;<br />Look on my Works, ye Mighty, and despair!<br />Nothing beside remains. Round the decay<br />Of that colossal Wreck, boundless and bare<br />The lone and level sands stretch far away.”<br /><br />Nothing beside remains to be said. I leave my earlier testimonies as a tribute to the process of discovering the truth of the storyteller in each (his)story:<br /><br />My predictions will face reality(or fiction?)-check now! I can't believe I am actually holding a brandnew copy of The Testaments in my hands... we are writing the date of 9/11 Anno Domini 2019.<br /><br />Let the journey begin!<br /><br />Thoughts on hearing it was coming in 2018:<br /><br />Based on the scary prophetic power of some of Margaret Atwood's other dystopian social studies, I am not sure I can even stomach the idea of what will happen to Offred next. <br /><br />May I suggest a feel-good start, changing her name into Nofred?<br /><br />No, that's not going to happen. I think the world is currently growing into Margaret Atwood's new novel, demonstrating the insanity a bit more each day. Getting very, very impatient by now. <br /><br />If I were to embrace a religion (No!), it would have to be the religion of literature, and I would praise the special god in Atwood's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262203.MaddAddam__MaddAddam___3_" title="MaddAddam (MaddAddam, #3) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">MaddAddam</a> every day by now: "Oh Fuck", as Snowman-the-Jimmy said only when it was really, really bad (which was quite often).</span>
  <a data-text-id="10664695810684827526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3215525041" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3215525041" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/77602709-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1519184646p2/77602709.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3215525041?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/77602709-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3215525041" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16561832641707581467"><b>3.4</b>/5 Stars ⭐️⭐️⭐️<br/><br/><blockquote>“You don’t believe the sky is falling until a chunk of it falls on you.”</blockquote><br/><br/>I'm very conflicted. I don't know if this book tricked me into liking it or if I actually do or don't. Because when I think about the characters and the plot a little more, I find problems. I'm very confused though. <br/><br/>This was a very anticipated and surprising book for many of us. We all know it happened because of the tv show but luckily they concealed it by developing a completely different plot with differ</span>
  <span id="freeText16561832641707581467" style="display:none"><b>3.4</b>/5 Stars ⭐️⭐️⭐️<br /><br /><blockquote>“You don’t believe the sky is falling until a chunk of it falls on you.”</blockquote><br /><br />I'm very conflicted. I don't know if this book tricked me into liking it or if I actually do or don't. Because when I think about the characters and the plot a little more, I find problems. I'm very confused though. <br /><br />This was a very anticipated and surprising book for many of us. We all know it happened because of the tv show but luckily they concealed it by developing a completely different plot with different characters that connected somehow to the original ones. Yes, <b>because they could doesn't mean they should</b>... but it was a story that indeed could be told. But it <b>ruined the mystery that first book left in us</b> you know? There is a trend in media these days with patronizing the fans and explaining to us through exposition every single thing like we're imbeciles. I love media which keeps all the mystery and lure you in without making everything blatantly obvious. This book explained too many things.<br /><br />First of all, there are three different perspectives we see the world through here. <i>"Baby Nicole"</i>, the <i>Famous Aunt Lydia</i> and a random but not so much at the end daughter of a commander, <i>Agnes</i>. Aunt Lydia's side was the most engaging and interesting to me. Yes, I have watched the show, yes, I fucking hate that bitch, but we see a different side of her here that I didn't anticipate. I will definitely be seeing her with a different light once the show is back on. <br /><br /><b>I found Nicole and Agnes to have the same voice</b> and were kind of annoying sometimes. At first, I needed like half a page to understand which POV I was reading from. Especially with scenes when they ware together. I feel like Nicole gained more character when she went to Gilead, but this was just because she was different than all of them. Yes, I enjoyed the swears. Duh. But I wanted something more character wise? I didn't feel as whole as with June/Offred. She was such an engaging character. Maybe this happened because we had many POVs to read from? I can't say.<br /><br />The plot felt somewhat... basic and expected? The book was definitely bigger than it should be. Too much exposition man. It wasn't boring at all for me... but I think the length could be lowered. <br /><br />I don't know what to tell you, people. I'm still very puzzled. Read this book, of course, but don't have the expectation that you'll like it as much as the first one. But I gotta admit, <b>this book gave me a big thirst for the show</b> now. I can't wait for it to be back, honestly. Even though there we are in a completely different path. I want to see if anything will stay the same once we get more seasons and when it ends. Anyway, till the next one K BYE!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16561832641707581467" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2981731794" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2981731794" />
    <a title="Ariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6536286-ariel"><img alt="Ariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530557165p2/6536286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2981731794?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ariel" href="/user/show/6536286-ariel">Ariel</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2981731794" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8322066195207393445">I liked this one more than A Handmaid's Tale! Call me crazy! But I loved that there was this exciting plot that was pushing the momentum forwards and that we were learning more about Gilead and the world outside and the way it came to be. I read this DIRECTLY after A Handmaid's Tale, like I read both of them in the span of a few days, and I felt this enriched the world so much more. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3043027625" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3043027625" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464987365p2/16254355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3043027625?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780525590460" href="/book/show/45165691-the-testaments">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=post-apocalyptic-dystopian">post-apocalyptic-dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=library">library</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3043027625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1134879127233716301">3.5 to 4 stars<br/><br/>I was going to go with 3 stars because while I liked it, it did not impress me as much as the first book. But, after discussing the book with my wife at dinner last night, I realized I got more from it than I thought, so I am upping my rating a bit.<br/><br/>In the afterword, Atwood hints at the fact that this was written in response to the recently renewed popularity of the first book and the current state of affairs in America. Because of this, it does kind of read like it was written to a</span>
  <span id="freeText1134879127233716301" style="display:none">3.5 to 4 stars<br /><br />I was going to go with 3 stars because while I liked it, it did not impress me as much as the first book. But, after discussing the book with my wife at dinner last night, I realized I got more from it than I thought, so I am upping my rating a bit.<br /><br />In the afterword, Atwood hints at the fact that this was written in response to the recently renewed popularity of the first book and the current state of affairs in America. Because of this, it does kind of read like it was written to appease the fans instead of an original sequel that she came up with all on her own. I am not saying this as a good or bad thing, it’s just how it felt to me – some people really like this sort of thing, so it may be perfect for you.<br /><br />I really enjoyed the first half to two-thirds of the book. At first, I was not sure how much I would remember about the first book since it has been about three years since I read it. Also, I have not been watching the TV show. But, the book did a pretty good job getting me back into the story and I thought the story and character development were both very good.<br /><br />However . . . with only about 25% of the book left, for me it got kind of “Hollywood” – a bit cheesy and unbelievable. Suddenly the characters were making odd choices that felt like they served no purpose other that to move the book quickly to a resolution. To have such a rushed and far-fetched resolution to such a richly developed story/dystopian world felt kind of weird. This is where the book lost points for me.<br /><br />I think that fans of the book and TV show should give this one a try and likely enjoy it. However, be prepared to find it a bit lacking compared to the first book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1134879127233716301" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3113791821" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3113791821" />
    <a title="jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10171516-jessica"><img alt="jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509983330p2/10171516.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3113791821?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jessica" href="/user/show/10171516-jessica">jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3113791821" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer140734155358351098">i know ive said this before, but just because an author <i>can</i> write a novel, does that really mean they <i>should?</i> i thought <i>‘the handmaids tale’</i> was strong enough on its own. yes, i had questions afterwards, but i like that the story is a reflection on real society, where we dont have all the answers. <br/><br/>and even though i did enjoy this solely from an entertainment perspective, i just dont find it necessary. i think atwood got caught up in all the success of the television adaptation and thought she co</span>
  <span id="freeText140734155358351098" style="display:none">i know ive said this before, but just because an author <i>can</i> write a novel, does that really mean they <i>should?</i> i thought <i>‘the handmaids tale’</i> was strong enough on its own. yes, i had questions afterwards, but i like that the story is a reflection on real society, where we dont have all the answers. <br /><br />and even though i did enjoy this solely from an entertainment perspective, i just dont find it necessary. i think atwood got caught up in all the success of the television adaptation and thought she could capitalise on writing a sequel. which i cant blame her for one bit - i would probably do the same. but again, this feels like a story that was forced to be written, not one that came organically or from inspiration. <br /><br />its not even told similarly to the first book. while this sequel also (rightly) calls out todays society for the oppression of women, it feels like the narrative is telling me what to think, where <i>‘the handmaids tale’</i> forced me to come to my own conclusions. i dunno. its just a major stylistic/writing change (downgrade, even) that didnt make the story any better, in my opinion. <br /><br />i do think those who find the world of gilead morbidly fascinating will enjoy this sequel and the new characters but, for me, im just left with the question of <i>‘why?’</i> which i unfortunately couldnt seem to get past.<br /><br /><b>↠ <i>2.5 stars</i></b></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2908953360" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2908953360" />
    <a title="Meike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59050228-meike"><img alt="Meike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514995700p2/59050228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2908953360?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meike" href="/user/show/59050228-meike">Meike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=canada">canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=2019-booker">2019-booker</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2908953360" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9534311183936846295"><b>Winner of the Booker Prize 2019 (together with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41081373.Girl__Woman__Other' title='Girl, Woman, Other by Bernardine Evaristo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Girl, Woman, Other</a>)</b><br/>This is a flashy, placative, but also intelligent thriller, here to make some points about society and to entertain - it's certainly not the most layered or subtle literature ever written, but you know what? It's engaging, rather suspenseful and great fun to read, full of quips and commentary on the world we live in, and sometimes, that's more than enough. And honestly: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale' title='The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Handmaid's Tale</a> wasn't particularly ambiguous or enigmati</span>
  <span id="freeText9534311183936846295" style="display:none"><b>Winner of the Booker Prize 2019 (together with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41081373.Girl__Woman__Other" title="Girl, Woman, Other by Bernardine Evaristo" rel="nofollow noopener">Girl, Woman, Other</a>)</b><br />This is a flashy, placative, but also intelligent thriller, here to make some points about society and to entertain - it's certainly not the most layered or subtle literature ever written, but you know what? It's engaging, rather suspenseful and great fun to read, full of quips and commentary on the world we live in, and sometimes, that's more than enough. And honestly: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a> wasn't particularly ambiguous or enigmatic either. <br /><br />As we already know from the book's predecessor, the theocratic terror regime of Gilead did fall - the book ended with a historic symposium on the failed state. "The Testaments" now tells us how this downfall came about, and we hear the story from the alternating perspectives of three women: The infamous and powerful Aunt Lydia from #1, who is one of the women who helped develop the misogynist rules and rites of Gileadean society; a teenage girl who grows up in Gilead and is supposed to submit to her role as a women without any rights; and another young girl who lives in Canada (which borders to Gilead) and discovers her family's connection to Mayday, the resistance group that aims to save women and bring down the vicious regime. In case you are now wondering what happened to Offred, the handmaid at the center of #1, let me tell you that all of the characters are somehow connected to her - beware, readers, it does not make much sense to start "The Testaments" before reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a> first. <br /><br />Atwood does a great job addressing all kinds of current issues within the narrative: Not only the misogyny of the current US President is lurking between the lines, there are also parts that refer to ISIS, the refugee crisis at state borders and in the Mediterranean, xenophobia and the lack of empathy and solidarity. Another important topic is that of opportunism: We learn how Aunt Lydia became an instrumental part in a machine that systemtically exploits and violates women, and as we all know, it's the mass of enablers who keep such machines running, not those at the very top. Just like in #1, the threat of fascism is at the core of the whole story: When inventing Gilead, Atwood was inspired by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11026128-tageb-cher-1924-1945" rel="nofollow noopener">the diaries of Joseph Goebbels</a>, and the appearence of the women in the book was influenced by the aesthetics of Leni Riefenstahl's propaganda film "Triumph of the Will". <br /><br />And yes, Atwood paints with very broad brush strokes: This book is highly accessible, and readers aren't required to do much work themselves. I also suppose that the enormous marketing campaign put some people off and, in the eyes of quite a few readers, compromised the novel as a work of "serious" literature. I have to say that I don't really mind though: If "serious" movies with world-class actors can have major premiere events with red carpets, fancy dresses, press frenzy and all, why shouldn't a world-class writer like Margaret Atwood live it up at Waterstones London with her gang of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9399.Jeanette_Winterson" title="Jeanette Winterson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jeanette Winterson</a> and freakin' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman" title="Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">Neil Gaiman</a> as well as people dressed as handmaids and Pearl Girls while the whole literary world watches? More power to you, Ms Atwood! <br /><br />So if you expect intricately crafted, subtly plotted, lyrically written prose, or a completely new twist on the whole Gilead saga, this novel will probably disappoint you. But if you want to read a straightfoward, intelligent, well-paced, witty thriller spiced with social commentary in which women take down the patriarchy, this is the book for you. This text has the potential to reach many readers who normally wouldn't pick up a book on feminism, and it will allow people to join the conversation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9534311183936846295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3099628630" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3099628630" />
    <a title="Melissa ~ Bantering Books" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33853154-melissa-bantering-books"><img alt="Melissa ~ Bantering Books" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584398324p2/33853154.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3099628630?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa ~ Bantering Books" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa ~ Bantering Books" href="/user/show/33853154-melissa-bantering-books">Melissa ~ Bantering Books</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3099628630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11212735380657786089"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale' title='The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Handmaid's Tale</a> is a modern classic. It is an important novel. It is a novel that I believe should be read by every woman at least once in her lifetime.<br/><br/>I hold it in the utmost regard, as I do Margaret Atwood. I marvel at her wisdom. I admire her intelligence. I wonder at her writing.<br/><br/>Because of my own personal appreciation for Atwood and <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i>, I have delayed reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975414.The_Testaments__The_Handmaid_s_Tale___2_' title='The Testaments (The Handmaid's Tale, #2) by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Testaments</a>. I have not wanted to be disappointed, even though deep down I knew it would be inevitable, to som</span>
  <span id="freeText11212735380657786089" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a> is a modern classic. It is an important novel. It is a novel that I believe should be read by every woman at least once in her lifetime.<br /><br />I hold it in the utmost regard, as I do Margaret Atwood. I marvel at her wisdom. I admire her intelligence. I wonder at her writing.<br /><br />Because of my own personal appreciation for Atwood and <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i>, I have delayed reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42975414.The_Testaments__The_Handmaid_s_Tale___2_" title="The Testaments (The Handmaid's Tale, #2) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Testaments</a>. I have not wanted to be disappointed, even though deep down I knew it would be inevitable, to some extent. Because how could Atwood write a follow-up that could even compare to the brilliance of the original? <br /><br />Many of the critics of this novel (and there have been MANY) feel that Atwood should never have ventured back into the world of Gilead. They strongly believe that the ambiguous ending of <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i> is perfection and should not have been touched. To be fair, I don’t entirely disagree with this sentiment. I love the ending, as well, and have always felt satisfied with it.<br /><br />But I am not one to ever bite the hand that feeds me. More Gilead? Please and thank you. <br /><br />So, it was with realistically adjusted expectations that I read <i>The Testaments</i>. I crossed my fingers, held my breath, and hoped that I would at least <i>like</i> the novel.<br /><br />And I did like it. I enjoyed <i>The Testaments</i> immensely. My worry was needless. <br /><br />I do believe <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i> will forever be the superior of the two novels, but without a doubt, Atwood has given us a worthy sequel.<br /><br />Set fifteen years after the closing scene of <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i>, with the totalitarian patriarchal theocracy of the Republic of Gilead still standing, the story of <i>The Testaments</i> is told through the viewpoints of three different women. Two of the three women are teenagers – Agnes, the daughter of a Commander in Gilead, and Daisy, a young woman living in Canada who witnesses the terrors of Gilead from afar. The third point of view is that of the infamous Aunt Lydia, inarguably the most influential, merciless woman in Gilead.<br /><br />The lives of the three women eventually intertwine, as they each discover the extent of all they are willing to sacrifice in order to right the world.<br /><br />Of the three viewpoints, Aunt Lydia is, by far, the most compelling voice. The chapters devoted to her are riveting. Through her secret writings, we are treated to an in-depth glimpse of the earlier fall of the United States and the methods used to capture and then convert the women to the belief system of the Sons of Jacob, the founders of the new world. We are also privy to the inner workings of Gilead and the machinations of both its male and female leadership.<br /><br />The testimonies of Agnes and Daisy, while mostly interesting, do seem to pale in comparison to that of Aunt Lydia. It is clear who is the true star of the story.<br /><br />But all three narrative threads succeed in one regard -- the personal stories of Aunt Lydia, Agnes, and Daisy will make you angry. The physical, emotional, and sexual abuse that is doled out by the society of Gilead is horrifying. (Sensitive readers, beware.) I felt that old, familiar anger returning while reading <i>The Testaments</i>. The exact same anger <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i> instilled in me. An anger that has never fully dissipated.<br /><br />An anger that, I think, speaks to the power of Atwood’s writing and the memorable world of Gilead that she has created.<br /><br />I feel I should warn that <i>The Testaments</i> will likely not answer all the lingering questions you may have from <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i>. Atwood leaves us with a vague understanding of the remainder of Offred’s life, but there are still many gaps left for our imaginations to fill. We must make peace with that fact.<br /><br />But this time, even though we may not have all the answers we desire, we at least have hope. Atwood closes the novel on a much more optimistic note than she did in <i>The Handmaid’s Tale</i>. And once again, the ending feels so very satisfying.<br /><br />For hope is beneficial to us all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11212735380657786089" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2992163320" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2992163320" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2992163320?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=usa-and-canada">usa-and-canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=unreliable-narrators">unreliable-narrators</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=god-religion-faith">god-religion-faith</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2992163320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5835263278308391455"><b>Why?</b> <br/><br/>Why did Atwood write this more than thirty years after <b>The Handmaid’s Tale</b> (see my review <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23945013' rel='nofollow noopener'>HERE</a>), when she’d already written sequels to that for the TV series? Because “<i>we started moving towards Gilead instead of away from it – particularly in the United States</i>” and more specifically, to answer “<i>How did Gilead fall?</i>” and “<i>How do you get to be such a person [as Aunt Lydia]?</i>”.<br/><br/>Why read it? Hype, a heavy discount, and morbid curiosity. It’s an easy read and enough of a page-turner to finish quic</span>
  <span id="freeText5835263278308391455" style="display:none"><b>Why?</b> <br><br>Why did Atwood write this more than thirty years after <b>The Handmaid’s Tale</b> (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23945013" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>), when she’d already written sequels to that for the TV series? Because “<i>we started moving towards Gilead instead of away from it – particularly in the United States</i>” and more specifically, to answer “<i>How did Gilead fall?</i>” and “<i>How do you get to be such a person [as Aunt Lydia]?</i>”.<br><br>Why read it? Hype, a heavy discount, and morbid curiosity. It’s an easy read and enough of a page-turner to finish quickly. It answers plenty of questions - and raises more. <br><b>No spoilers below.</b> <br><br>But, it’s a weird hybrid. It’s almost as if she’d planned adult and YA versions, then wove them into a single plait. <br><br><b>The Decline and Fall…? </b><br><br>“<i>Per ardua cum estrus</i>”<br><br>The plot is predictable, the writing is full of clichés (excusable for Biblical homilies and Gilead sayings), and the more delicately variegated symbolism of Handmaid (especially red and tulips) degrades to more heavy handed mentions of flowers (usually blue ones - the colour for wives). <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1570484394i/28259536._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>Tulips and forget-me-nots</i> (<a href="https://www.maxpixel.net" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br>Overall, it felt lazy, yet I eagerly read it to the end. It’s better than the unnecessary <b>MaddAddam</b> (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/672699336" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>) and the execrable <b>The Heart Goes Last</b> (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1422805780" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>). <br><br><b>Old and Wise or Young and Annoying?</b><br><br>There are three narrators, in short alternating chapters, mostly concerning events around 15 years after the end of Handmaid. It was obvious how and when the threads would cross and eventually join. <br><br>The “Ardua Hall Holograph” was secretly written by Aunt Lydia, as events unfolded, though with relevant backstory about her life before Gilead. The TV series had already presented a more rounded, complex, and sympathetic view of her than the original novel, and this expands on that. It’s <b>4*</b>. <br><br>“<i>He owes me, but that could prove a liability. Some people do not enjoy being indebted.</i>”<br><br>Aunt Lydia is strong, shrewd, knowledgeable, and ruthless. She’s the chief Aunt, but no one expects a woman to do all that she does or know all that she knows; her increasing power goes unnoticed. She knows the value of information; she plots, and bides her time. I was reminded of Hilary Mantel’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6101138.Wolf_Hall__Thomas_Cromwell___1_" title="Wolf Hall (Thomas Cromwell, #1) by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolf Hall</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13507212.Bring_Up_the_Bodies__Thomas_Cromwell___2_" title="Bring Up the Bodies (Thomas Cromwell, #2) by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Bring Up the Bodies</a> (which I confess I saw on TV, rather than read).<br><br>The other sections are “Transcripts of Witness Testimony” by two different women, describing their teen years, apparently written years after the events described. Agnes, the wide-eyed innocent child of a commander in Gilead, and Daisy, a bit of a know-it-all, living free in Canada.<br><br>After a dramatic event, the change in Daisy’s character and voice is disproportionate. She becomes an insufferable teen, complete with irrelevant crush. Her adult self alters tone accordingly. As the end of the story approaches, the mood and intended audience feels increasingly like <b>The Hunger Games</b> (see my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/177445520" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>) and its ilk. Not my thing, so I rate these narrations <b>2*</b>.<br><br><b>Contemporary Shadows?</b><br><br>All three women have at least two very different roles and identities during their lives. The different names are not confusing, but the switching is sometimes inconsistent and thus distracting. All three find risky ways to resist the patriarchy and to stand up for women. <br><br>This is written in the light (dark?) of <b>#MeToo</b> as much and the resurgence of religious-backed populist demagogues. Atwood is clearly on the side of women, and against authoritarian regimes, but I expect her readers already are. Unlike Handmaid, she doesn’t have anything very new to say, despite increased emphasis on bloodlines and whether the “real” mother is the one who gave birth or the one who raised and loved the child. <br><br><b>Humour?</b><br><br>For reasons I cannot fathom, Peter Kemp classed this as comedy in <a href="https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/the-testaments-by-margaret-atwood-review-tale-of-the-unexpected-cctp3b03z" rel="nofollow noopener">his review</a> for The Times. There was a puerile pun (the Aunts say "Pen Is Envy", because other women can’t read or write), but other than that…? <br><br><b>Rapid Reading?</b><br><br>I read this quickly for a book of more than 400 pages. More impressively, supplicant aunts, totally illiterate when they arrive in their mid teens, are fluent readers of adult books in less than six months, with a basic knowledge of geography too, even though most of each day is filled with arduous chores. As a former primary school teacher, I’d love to know how! (All we’re told is that they start off with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dick_and_Jane" rel="nofollow noopener">Dick and Jane</a> books, whose illustrations had the clothes made more modest.)<br><br>Meanwhile, Aunt Lydia’s favourite books are not quick reads: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324130" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Eyre</a>, Anna Karenina, Tess of the d’Urbervilles, Paradise Lost, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1468790961" rel="nofollow noopener">Lives of Girls and Women</a>. Quite a tribute to Alice Munro from her compatriot!<br><br><b>Unreliable?</b><br><br>"<i>History does not repeat itself, but it rhymes.</i>" <br><br>Like Handmaid, this ends with a Symposium of Gileadean Studies in the far future, discussing the newly-found testimonies (cassette tapes in Handmaid, documents here). It’s the first time we hear directly from a man. <br><br>He questions the authenticity and accuracy - as men often do with women’s testimonies. But it’s important in an academic context, dealing with a regime that ruled with fear and relentless propaganda, and where few women were able to write. Aunt Lydia had plenty of reasons justify her role in the regime, so her embroidery may not be of the petit-point variety that future wives were taught. But Atwood didn’t leave enough doubt to make it worth raising the question. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1570484394i/28259537.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>Petit-point embroidered blue flowers</i> (<a href="ebay.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>.)<br><br><b>Redemption?</b><br><br>For a book that is firmly against a perverted version of Christianity, redemption is a strong element. <br>•	Aunt Lydia wants to redeem herself in the eyes of possible future readers. <br>•	One person demonstrates the greatest love (<a href="https://biblehub.com/john/15-13.htm" rel="nofollow noopener">John 15:13</a>), yet we never hear from them directly. <br>•	By the time I finished this review, Atwood had redeemed herself in my eyes. I am still a fan, though I prefer her earlier works. <br><br><b>Quotes</b><br><br>•	“She was no longer a precious flower, but a much more dangerous creature.” [Post puberty]<br><br>•	“The body has its twitches, which it can be humiliating as well as rewarding to obey.” [Sex]<br><br>•	“We were precious flowers that had to be kept safely inside glass houses, or else we would be ambushed and our petals would be torn off and oru treasure would be stolen and we would be ripped apart and trampled by the ravenous men who might lurk around any corner, out there in the wide sharp-edged sin-ridden world.” [Bit of a mouthful!]<br><br>•	“The crocuses have melted, the daffodils have shrivelled to paper, the tulips have performed their enticing dance, flipping their petal skirts inside out before dropping them completely.”<br><br>•	“Bibles brooded in the darkness of their locked boxes, glowing with arcane energy.”<br><br>•	“Melanie had a distant smell… Like a floral guest soap in a strange house… She didn’t smell to me like my mother.”<br><br>•	Family photos, “as if they lived their lives twice, once in reality and the second time for the photo.”<br><br>•	“I feared I might lose my faith. If you’ve never had a faith, you will not understand… You feel as if your best friend is dying… The world was emptying itself of meaning.”<br><br>•	“All that festers is not gold, but it can be made profitable in non-monetary ways.”<br><br>•	“Gratitude is valuable to me. I like to bank it.”<br><br>•	“Any forced change of leadership is always followed by a move to rush the opposition.”<br><br>•	"How tedious is a tyranny in the throes of enactment. It's always the same plot."<br><br>•	“It has an acid smell, fear. It’s corrosive.”<br><br>•	“Terror does not exactly reign. Instead it paralyses.”<br><br>•	“You’d be surprised who quickly the mind goes soggy in the absence of other people. One person alone is not a full person: we exist in relation to others.”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1570484394i/28259538._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Image</b>: <i>Empty cradle</i> (<a>Source</a>.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="5835263278308391455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
